<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1134653256">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">

<div class="side-box-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Example.of.complex.text.rendering.svg/40px-Example.of.complex.text.rendering.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="19" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Example.of.complex.text.rendering.svg/60px-Example.of.complex.text.rendering.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Example.of.complex.text.rendering.svg/80px-Example.of.complex.text.rendering.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="58" data-file-height="28" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Brahmic_scripts" title="Brahmic scripts">Indic text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support_(Indic)" title="Help:Multilingual support (Indic)">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Mojibake" title="Mojibake">question marks or boxes</a>, misplaced vowels or missing conjuncts&#32;instead of Indic text.</div></div>
</div>
<p><b>Awadhi</b> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">Hindi pronunciation:</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="hi-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hindi_and_Urdu" title="Help:IPA/Hindi and Urdu">&#91;əʋ.d̪ʱi&#93;</a></span>; <span title="Hindi-language text"><span lang="hi">अवधी</span></span>), also known as <b>Audhi</b><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> (<span title="Hindi-language text"><span lang="hi">औधी</span></span>), is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Aryan language">Indo-Aryan language</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Awadh" title="Awadh">Awadh</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Terai" title="Terai">Terai region</a> of western <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The name <i>Awadh</i> is connected to <a href="/wiki/Ayodhya" title="Ayodhya">Ayodhya</a>, the ancient city, which is regarded as the homeland of the Hindu god <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a>.  It was, along with <a href="/wiki/Braj_Bhasha" title="Braj Bhasha">Braj</a>, used widely as a literary vehicle before being displaced by <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguistically, Awadhi is a language at par with <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a>. However, it is regarded by the Indian government to be a dialect of Hindi, and the area where Awadhi is spoken to be a part of the Hindi-language area owing to their cultural proximity, meanwhile Standard Hindi also serves as the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a><sup id="cite_ref-fulllangdatacensus_2011_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fulllangdatacensus_2011-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> of the region. As a result, <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, rather than Awadhi, is used for school instruction as well as administrative and official purposes; and its literature falls within the scope of <a href="/wiki/Hindi_literature" title="Hindi literature">Hindi literature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternative names of Awadhi include <i>Baiswāri</i> (after the subregion of <a href="/wiki/Baiswara" title="Baiswara">Baiswara</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> as well as the sometimes ambiguous <i>Pūrbī</i>, literally meaning "eastern", and <i>Kōsalī</i> (named after the ancient <a href="/wiki/Kosala" title="Kosala">Kosala Kingdom</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_India">In India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: In India"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Awadhi is predominantly spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Awadh" title="Awadh">Awadh</a> region encompassing central <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, along with the lower part of the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganga</a>-<a href="/wiki/Yamuna" title="Yamuna">Yamuna</a> <a href="/wiki/Doab" title="Doab">doab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In the west, it is bounded by Western Hindi, specifically <a href="/wiki/Kannauji_language" title="Kannauji language">Kannauji</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bundeli_language" title="Bundeli language">Bundeli</a>, while in the east, <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri_language" title="Bhojpuri language">Bhojpuri</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Bihari_languages" title="Bihari languages">Bihari</a> group of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Eastern Indo-Aryan languages">Eastern Indo-Aryan languages</a> is spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>  In the north, it is bounded by the country of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and in the south by <a href="/wiki/Bagheli_language" title="Bagheli language">Bagheli</a>, which shares a great resemblance with Awadhi.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The following districts of North and Central UP speak Awadhi-
</p>
<ul><li>Kanpur (along with <a href="/wiki/Kannauji_language" title="Kannauji language">Kannauji</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lakhimpur_Kheri_district" title="Lakhimpur Kheri district">Lakhimpur Kheri</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sitapur_district" title="Sitapur district">Sitapur</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hardoi_district" title="Hardoi district">Hardoi</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/Kannauji_language" title="Kannauji language">Kannauji</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Unnao_district" title="Unnao district">Unnao</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fatehpur_district" title="Fatehpur district">Fatehpur</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Barabanki_district" title="Barabanki district">Barabanki</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lucknow_district" title="Lucknow district">Lucknow</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Raebareli_district" title="Raebareli district">Rae Bareli</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amethi_district" title="Amethi district">Amethi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bahraich_district" title="Bahraich district">Bahraich</a></li></ul>
<p>In eastern parts of UP the Awadhi language changes its form to a special dialect called "Eastern Standard Awadhi." This region makes boundary with <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhojpuri">Bhojpuri</a> speaking districts of Purvanchal. This part include districts of-
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ayodhya_district" title="Ayodhya district">Ayodhya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ambedkar_Nagar_district" title="Ambedkar Nagar district">Ambedkar Nagar</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prayagraj_district" title="Prayagraj district">Prayagraj</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jaunpur_district" title="Jaunpur district">Jaunpur</a> (western parts)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mirzapur_district" title="Mirzapur district">Mirzapur</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bhadohi_district" title="Bhadohi district">Bhadohi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sultanpur_district" title="Sultanpur district">Sultanpur</a>(western part)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pratapgarh_district,_Uttar_Pradesh" title="Pratapgarh district, Uttar Pradesh">Pratapgarh</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gonda_district" title="Gonda district">Gonda</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Basti_district" title="Basti district">Basti</a> (western parts)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siddharthnagar_district" title="Siddharthnagar district">Siddharthnagar</a> (western parts)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kaushambi_district" title="Kaushambi district">Kaushambi</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Nepal">In Nepal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: In Nepal"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Language_Commission_(Nepal)" title="Language Commission (Nepal)">Language Commission of Nepal</a> has recommended <a href="/wiki/Tharu_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tharu language">Tharu</a> and Awadhi as official language in Lumbini province.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Awadhi is spoken in two provinces in Nepal:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lumbini_Province" title="Lumbini Province">Lumbini Province</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Banke_District" title="Banke District">Banke District</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bardiya_District" title="Bardiya District">Bardiya District</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dang_District,_Nepal" title="Dang District, Nepal">Dang District</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kapilvastu_District" title="Kapilvastu District">Kapilvastu District</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sudurpashchim_Province" title="Sudurpashchim Province">Sudurpashchim Province</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kailali_District" title="Kailali District">Kailali District</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kanchanpur_District" title="Kanchanpur District">Kanchanpur District</a></li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_South_Asia">Outside South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Outside South Asia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Hindi" title="Fiji Hindi">Fiji Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Hindustani" title="Caribbean Hindustani">Caribbean Hindustani</a></div>
<p>A language influenced by Awadhi (as well as other languages) is also spoken as a <i>lingua franca</i> for <a href="/wiki/Indo-Fijians" title="Indo-Fijians">Indians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a> and is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Hindi" title="Fiji Hindi">Fijian Hindi</a>. According to <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i>, it is a type of Awadhi influenced by <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri_language" title="Bhojpuri language">Bhojpuri</a> and is also classified as Eastern-Hindi.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Hindustani" title="Caribbean Hindustani">Caribbean Hindustani</a> spoken by <a href="/wiki/Indo-Caribbeans" title="Indo-Caribbeans">Indians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a> is based on Bhojpuri and partly on Awadhi. The Hindustani that is spoken in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and the Bhojpuri spoken in <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> is also partly influenced by Awadhi.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Awadhi_classification.png" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Awadhi_classification.png/220px-Awadhi_classification.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Awadhi_classification.png/330px-Awadhi_classification.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/Awadhi_classification.png 2x" data-file-width="404" data-file-height="293" /></a><figcaption>Linguistic classification of Awadhi language.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Awadhi is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European language</a> and belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a> sub-group of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a> language family. Within the Indo-Aryan <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, it falls under the East-Central zone of languages and is often recognised as Eastern-Hindi. It is generally believed that an older form of <a href="/wiki/Ardhamagadhi_Prakrit" title="Ardhamagadhi Prakrit">Ardhamagadhi</a>, which agreed partly with <a href="/wiki/Shauraseni_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Shauraseni language">Sauraseni</a> and partly with <a href="/wiki/Magadhi_Prakrit" title="Magadhi Prakrit">Magadhi Prakrit</a>, could be the basis of Awadhi.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The closest relative of Awadhi is the <a href="/wiki/Bagheli_language" title="Bagheli language">Bagheli language</a> as genealogically both descend from the same 'Half-Magadhi'. Most early Indian linguists regarded Bagheli merely as 'the southern form of Awadhi', but recent studies accept Bagheli as a separate dialect at par with Awadhi and not merely a sub-dialect of it.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Literature"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Awadhi-language_poets" title="List of Awadhi-language poets">List of Awadhi-language poets</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late-medieval_and_early-modern_India">Late-medieval and early-modern India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Late-medieval and early-modern India"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In this period, Awadhi became the vehicle for <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poetry</a> in northern India.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Its literature is mainly divided into: bhaktīkāvya (devotional poetry) and premākhyān (romantic tales).
</p>
<h4><span id="Bhakt.C4.ABk.C4.81vyas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhaktīkāvyas">Bhaktīkāvyas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bhaktīkāvyas"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The most important work, probably in any modern <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan language</a>, came from the poet-saint <a href="/wiki/Tulsidas" title="Tulsidas">Tulsidas</a> in the form of <i><a href="/wiki/Ramcharitmanas" title="Ramcharitmanas">Ramcharitmanas</a></i> (1575 C.E.) or "The Lake of the Deeds of <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a>", written in <a href="/wiki/Doha_(poetry)" title="Doha (poetry)">doha</a>-<a href="/wiki/Chaupai_(poetry)" title="Chaupai (poetry)">chaupai</a> <a href="/wiki/Metre_(poetry)" title="Metre (poetry)">metre</a>. Its plot is mostly derivative, either from the original <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Rāmāyaṇa</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Valmiki" title="Valmiki">Valmiki</a> or from the <i><a href="/wiki/Adhyatma_Ramayana" title="Adhyatma Ramayana">Adhyātma Rāmāyaṇa</a></i>, both of which are in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> had acclaimed the <i>Ramcharitmanas</i> as "the greatest book of all devotional literature" while western observers have christened it as "the Bible of <a href="/wiki/Northern_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern India">Northern India</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It is sometimes synonymously referred as 'Tulsidas Ramayana' or simply 'the Ramayana'.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti <p><a href="/wiki/Tulsidas" title="Tulsidas">Tulsidas</a>'s compositions <i><a href="/wiki/Hanuman_Chalisa" title="Hanuman Chalisa">Hanuman Chalisa</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <i>Pārvatī Maṅgala</i> and <i>Jānakī Maṅgala</i> are also written in Awadhi.<sup id="cite_ref-Saxena_1971_12_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saxena_1971_12-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1157697682">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em!important}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0.5em!important}}</style>
<table role="presentation" class="verse_translation" style="margin-left:1em !important">

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top">
<td><div style="font-style:roman;text-align:left" lang="awa" class="poem">
<p>अंडकोस प्रति प्रति निज रूपा।<br />
देखेउँ जिनस अनेक अनूपा॥<br />
<u>अवधपुरी</u> प्रति भुअन निनारी।<br />
<u>सरजू</u> भिन्न भिन्न नर नारी॥
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div style="font-style:roman;text-align:left" lang="" class="poem">
<p>In each universe I saw my own self,<br />
As well as many an object beyond compare;<br />
Each universe had its own <u>Ayodhya</u>,<br />
With its own <u>Saryu</u> and its own men and women.
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align:top;font-size:90%">
<td style="padding-left:1.6em;text-align:left">—<a href="/wiki/Tulsidas" title="Tulsidas">Tulsidas</a>, 7.81.3 chaupai, <a href="/wiki/Ramcharitmanas" title="Ramcharitmanas">Ramcharitmanas</a>
</td>
<td style="padding-left:3.6em;text-align:left">—Translation by R.C Prasad<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1157697682">
<table role="presentation" class="verse_translation" style="margin-left:1em !important">

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top">
<td><div style="font-style:roman;text-align:left" lang="awa" class="poem">
<p>सिंधु तीर एक भूधर सुंदर।<br />
कौतुक कूदि चढ़ेउ ता-ऊपर॥<br />
बार-बार रघुबीर सँभारी।<br />
तरकेउ <u>पवनतनय</u> बल भारी॥
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div style="font-style:roman;text-align:left" lang="" class="poem">
<p>On the sea-shore there was a mountain lovely,<br />
He hopped to its peak sportively;<br />
Over and again, the Lord he did recall<br />
And the <u>Son of Wind </u>darted with energy no small.
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="vertical-align:top;font-size:90%">
<td style="padding-left:1.6em;text-align:left">—<a href="/wiki/Tulsidas" title="Tulsidas">Tulsidas</a>, 5.1.3 chaupai, <a href="/wiki/Ramcharitmanas" title="Ramcharitmanas">Ramcharitmanas</a>
</td>
<td style="padding-left:3.6em;text-align:left">—Translation<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The first Hindi vernacular adaptation of the 'Dasam Skandha' of the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavata_Purana" title="Bhagavata Purana">Bhagavata Purana</a></i>, the "Haricharit" by Lalachdas, who hailed from Hastigram (present-day Hathgaon near <a href="/wiki/Raebareli_district" title="Raebareli district">Rae Bareilly</a>), was concluded in 1530 C.E. It circulated widely for a long time and scores of manuscript copies of the text have been found as far as eastern Uttar Pradesh and <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Malwa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, all written in the <a href="/wiki/Kaithi" title="Kaithi">Kaithi</a> script.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Satyavatī</i> (ca. 1501) of Ishvaradas (of Delhi) under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sikandar_Lodi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikandar Lodi">Sikander Lodi</a> and <i>Avadhabilāsa</i> (1700 C.E.) of Laladas were also written in Awadhi.
</p><p>Awadhi appeared as a major component in the works of <i>Bhakti</i> saints like <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a>, who used a language often described as being a <i>pancmel khicṛī</i> or "a hotch-potch" of several vernaculars.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The language of <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a>'s major work <i><a href="/wiki/Bijak" title="Bijak">Bijak</a></i> is primarily Awadhi.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Prem.C4.81khy.C4.81ns"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Premākhyāns">Premākhyāns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Premākhyāns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:192px;max-width:192px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader" style="background-color:#FDF5E6">Illustrations to Awadhi <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> texts</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:190px;max-width:190px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:306px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Nagamati_talks_to_her_parrot,_Padmavat,_c1750.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Queen_Nagamati_talks_to_her_parrot%2C_Padmavat%2C_c1750.jpg/188px-Queen_Nagamati_talks_to_her_parrot%2C_Padmavat%2C_c1750.jpg" decoding="async" width="188" height="306" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Queen_Nagamati_talks_to_her_parrot%2C_Padmavat%2C_c1750.jpg/282px-Queen_Nagamati_talks_to_her_parrot%2C_Padmavat%2C_c1750.jpg 1.5x, data-file-width="393" data-file-height="640" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Queen Nagamati talks to her parrot, <a href="/wiki/Padmavat" title="Padmavat">Padmavat</a>, 1750 C.E.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:190px;max-width:190px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:147px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg/188px-Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="188" height="148" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg/282px-Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg/376px-Meister_des_Madhu-Malati-Manuskripts_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="1237" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Lovers shoot at a tiger in the jungle. From the mystical Sufi text <a href="/wiki/Madhumalati" title="Madhumalati">Madhumalati</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Awadhi also emerged as the favourite literary language of the Eastern Sufis from the last quarter of the 14th century onwards. It became the language of <i>premākhyāns</i>, romantic tales built on the pattern of Persian <i><a href="/wiki/Mathnawi_(poetic_form)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathnawi (poetic form)">masnavi</a></i>, steeped in Sufi <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> but set in a purely Indian background, with a large number of <a href="/wiki/Motif_(narrative)" title="Motif (narrative)">motifs</a> directly borrowed from Indian lore. The first of such <i>premākhyān</i> in the Awadhi language was Candāyan (1379 C.E.) of Maulana Da'ud.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The tradition was carried forward by <a href="/wiki/Malik_Muhammad_Jayasi" title="Malik Muhammad Jayasi">Jayasi</a>, whose masterpiece, the <a href="/wiki/Padmavat" title="Padmavat">Padmāvat</a> (1540 C.E.) was composed under the reign of the famous ruler <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a>. The Padmavat travelled far and wide, from <a href="/wiki/Arakan" title="Arakan">Arakan</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan</a>, and was eagerly copied and retold in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other prominent works of <a href="/wiki/Malik_Muhammad_Jayasi" title="Malik Muhammad Jayasi">Jayasi</a>—Kānhāvat,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Akhrāvaṭ<sup id="cite_ref-Saxena_1971_12_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saxena_1971_12-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and Ākhrī Kalām<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> are also written in Awadhi.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>I'll tell you about my great town, the ever-beautiful Jais.<br />
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Satya_Yuga" title="Satya Yuga">satyayuga</a></i> it was a holy place, then it was called the "Town of
Gardens."<br />
Then the <i><a href="/wiki/Treta_Yuga" title="Treta Yuga">treta</a></i> went, and when the <i><a href="/wiki/Dvapara_Yuga" title="Dvapara Yuga">dvapara</a></i> came, there was a great rishi
called <i>Bhunjaraja</i>.<br />
88,000 rishis lived here then, and dense ... and eighty-four ponds.<br />
They baked bricks to make solid ghats, and dug eight-four wells.<br />
Here and there they built handsome forts, at night they looked like stars
in the sky.<br />
They also put up several orchards with temples on top.<br />
<br />
</p><p>
Doha: They sat there doing tapas, all those human<i> avataras</i>.They crossed this world doing <i>homa</i> and <i>japa </i>day and night.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite><a href="/wiki/Malik_Muhammad_Jayasi" title="Malik Muhammad Jayasi">Jayasi</a>, Kanhavat, ed. Pathak (8), 7–8.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The Awadhi romance Mirigāvatī (ca.1503) or "The Magic Doe", was written by Shaikh 'Qutban' Suhravardi, who was an expert and storyteller attached to the court-in-exile of Sultan Hussain Shah Sharqi of <a href="/wiki/Jaunpur_district" title="Jaunpur district">Jaunpur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Another romance named <a href="/wiki/Madhumalati" title="Madhumalati">Madhumālatī</a> or "Night Flowering Jasmine" by poet Sayyid Manjhan Rajgiri was written in 1545 C.E.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" title="Amir Khusrau">Amir Khusrau</a> (d. 1379 C.E) is also said to have written some compositions in Awadhi.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_India">Modern India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Modern India"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most significant contributions to the Awadhi literature in the modern period have come from writers like <a href="/wiki/Ramai_Kaka" title="Ramai Kaka">Ramai Kaka</a> (1915–1982 C.E.), Balbhadra Prasad Dikshit better known as ‘Padhees’(1898–1943 C.E.) and Vanshidhar Shukla (1904–1980 C.E.).  
</p><p>
‘Krishnayan’ (1942 C.E.) is a major Awadhi epic-poem that Dwarka Prasad Mishra wrote in imprisonment during the <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Freedom Movement of India</a>. In 2022 Dr. <a href="/wiki/Vidya_Vindu_Singh" title="Vidya Vindu Singh">Vidya Vindu Singh</a> has been awarded <a href="/wiki/Padma_Shri" title="Padma Shri">Padma Shri</a> for her contribution in Awadhi literature.</p><div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Awadhi possesses both voiced and <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">voiceless</a> vowels. The voiced vowels are: /ə/, /ʌ/, /aː/, /ɪ/, /iː/, /ʊ/, /uː/, /e/, /eː/, /o/, /oː/.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The voiceless vowels, also described as "whispered vowels" are: /i̥/, /ʊ̥/, /e̥/.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparative_grammar">Comparative grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Comparative grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Awadhi has many features that separate it from the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Western_Hindi" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Hindi">Western Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bihari_languages" title="Bihari languages">Bihari</a> vernaculars. In Awadhi, <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> are generally both short and long, whereas Western Hindi has generally short while Bihari generally employs longer and long forms. The <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a> is rigorously maintained in Western Hindi, Awadhi is a little loose yet largely preserved, while Bihari is highly attenuated. Regarding <a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">postpositions</a>, Awadhi is distinguished from Western Hindi by the absence of agentive postposition in the former, agreeing with Bihari dialects. The <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>-<a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a> postposition in Awadhi is /kaː/ or /kə/ while Western Hindi has /koː/ or /kɔː/ and Bihari has /keː/. The <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a> postposition in both Bihari and Western Hindi is /mẽː/ while Awadhi has /maː/. The <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a> in Awadhi have /toːɾ-/, /moːɾ-/ as personal genitives while /teːɾ-/, /meːɾ-/ are used in Western Hindi. The oblique of /ɦəmaːɾ/ is /ɦəmɾeː/ in Awadhi while it is /ɦəmaːɾeː/ in Western Hindi and /ɦəmrən'kæ/ in Bihari.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another defining characteristic of Awadhi is the <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affix</a> /-ɪs/ as in /dɪɦɪs/, /maːɾɪs/ etc. The neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Bhojpuri_language" title="Bhojpuri language">Bhojpuri</a> has the distinctive (i) /laː/ enclitic in <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present tense</a> (ii) /-l/ in <a href="/wiki/Past_tense" title="Past tense">past tense</a> (iii) dative postposition /-laː/ which separates it from the Awadhi language.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">Pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_formation">Word formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Word formation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Following are the morphological processes of stem formation in the Awadhi language:
</p><p><b>Affixation</b>
</p><p>An affix is used to alter the meaning or form of a word. It can be either a prefix or a suffix.
</p>
<ul><li>Example: Prefix <i>bē–</i> preceding the root <i>saram</i> means "shameless" while <i>apna</i> followed by <i>–pan</i> means "belonging-ness".</li></ul>
<p><b>Compounding</b>
</p><p>Two or more stems are combined to form one stem.
</p>
<ul><li>Example: <i>nīlkanṭh</i> means "blue bird" and <i>banmānus</i> means "forest man" or "chimpanzee".</li></ul>
<p><b>Reduplication</b>
</p><p>This process involves the repetition of certain forms. It may be complete, partial, or interrupted.
</p>
<ol><li><b>Complete reduplication:</b> It denotes continuity of action.
<ul><li>Example: <i>jāt-jāt</i> for "going on".</li></ul></li>
<li><b>Partial</b> <b>reduplication:</b> It denotes similarity of one object to other.
<ul><li>Example: <i>hãpaṭ-dãpaṭ</i> for "panting".</li></ul></li>
<li><b>Interrupted reduplication:</b> It stresses on the instant condition of the action that follows and expresses abundance of something.
<ul><li>Example: <i>khētaī khēt</i> "between the fields"; <i>garmaī garam</i> "the very hot".</li></ul></li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment">Entertainment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Entertainment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 1961 film <i><a href="/wiki/Gunga_Jumna" title="Gunga Jumna">Gunga Jumna</a></i> features Awadhi being spoken by the characters in a neutralised form. In the 2001 film <i><a href="/wiki/Lagaan" title="Lagaan">Lagaan</a></i>, a neutralised form of Awadhi language was used to make it understandable to audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The 2009 film <i><a href="/wiki/Dev.D" title="Dev.D">Dev.D</a></i> features an Awadhi song, "Paayaliya", composed by <a href="/wiki/Amit_Trivedi" title="Amit Trivedi">Amit Trivedi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Yudh_(TV_series)" title="Yudh (TV series)">Yudh</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Amitabh_Bachchan" title="Amitabh Bachchan">Amitabh Bachchan</a> spoke parts of his dialogue in Awadhi, which received critical acclaim from the <i><a href="/wiki/Hindustan_Times" title="Hindustan Times">Hindustan Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Awadhi is also spoken by the residents of <a href="/wiki/Ayodhya" title="Ayodhya">Ayodhya</a> and other minor characters in <a href="/wiki/Ramanand_Sagar" title="Ramanand Sagar">Ramanand Sagar</a>'s 1987 television series <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayan_(1987_TV_series)" title="Ramayan (1987 TV series)">Ramayan</a></i>. It is believed that the tune and lyrics of the song "<a href="/wiki/Rang_Barse_Bhige_Chunar_Wali" title="Rang Barse Bhige Chunar Wali">Rang Barse Bhige Chunar Wali</a>", from the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Silsila_(1981_film)" title="Silsila (1981 film)">Silsila</a></i> starring Amitabh Bachchan and <a href="/wiki/Rekha" title="Rekha">Rekha</a>, are taken from a <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Rajasthan" title="Music of Rajasthan">Rajasthani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Haryana" title="Music of Haryana">Haryanvi</a> folk <a href="/wiki/Bhajan" title="Bhajan">bhajan</a> about <a href="/wiki/Meera" class="mw-redirect" title="Meera">Meera</a>. However the lyrics are slightly altered into the Awadhi dialect of <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> to mould the song into appropriate context of the movie script. The Awadhi folk song "Mere Angne Mein Tumhara Kya Kaam Hai" has become popular in Bollywood with a neutralized version of it being in the 1981 film <i><a href="/wiki/Laawaris_(1981_film)" title="Laawaris (1981 film)">Laawaris</a></i> starring Amitabh Bachchan, as well as being in the 1970 film <i><a href="/wiki/Bombay_Talkie" title="Bombay Talkie">Bombay Talkie</a></i> and the 1975 film <i>Maze Le Lo</i>, it was also released as a single by <a href="/wiki/Neha_Kakkar" title="Neha Kakkar">Neha Kakkar</a> in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Another Awadhi folk song that became popular through Bollywood was "Holi Khele Raghuveera", which was neutralized and sung by Amitabh Bachchan and put into the 2003 film <i><a href="/wiki/Baghban_(2003_film)" title="Baghban (2003 film)">Baghban</a></i> starring Amitabh Bachchan and <a href="/wiki/Hema_Malini" title="Hema Malini">Hema Malini</a>. The hit 1994 Bollywood hit film <i><a href="/wiki/Hum_Aapke_Hain_Koun..!" title="Hum Aapke Hain Koun..!">Hum Aapke Hain Koun..!</a></i> is based on an Awadhi film from 1982 <i><a href="/wiki/Nadiya_Ke_Paar_(1982_film)" title="Nadiya Ke Paar (1982 film)">Nadiya Ke Paar</a></i>, which itself is partly based on the novel <i>Kohbar Ki Shart</i> by Keshav Prasad Mishra.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk">Folk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Folk"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The genres of folklore sung in Awadh include Sohar, Sariya, Byaah, Suhag, Gaari, Nakta, Banraa (Banna-Banni), Alha, Sawan, Jhula, Hori, Barahmasa, and <a href="/wiki/Kajari" title="Kajari">Kajri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_phrases">Sample phrases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Awadhi_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sample phrases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Awadhi language comes with its dialectal variations. For instance, in western regions, the auxiliary /hʌiː/ is used, while in central and eastern parts /ʌhʌiː/ is used.
</p><p>The following examples were taken from Baburam Saxena's <i>Evolution of Awadhi</i>, and alternative versions are also provided to show dialectal variations.
</p>
<table style="margin:auto;" class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th style="background:#ffdead;">English
</th>
<th style="background:#ffdead;">Awadhi (<a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a>)
</th>
<th style="background:#ffdead;">Awadhi (<a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a>)
</th></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td rowspan="2"><b>Who were there?</b>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɦʊãː<span class="wrap"> </span>koː<span class="wrap"> </span>or<span class="wrap"> </span>kəʊn<span class="wrap"> </span>ɾəɦəĩ</span>
</td>
<td><span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">हुआँ को (कउन) रहें?</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td><i><b>alt</b>.</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɦʊãː<span class="wrap"> </span>keː<span class="wrap"> </span>or<span class="wrap"> </span>kəʊn<span class="wrap"> </span>ɾəɦəin</span>
</td>
<td><i><b>alt</b></i>. <span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">हुआँ के/कउन रहेन?</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td rowspan="2"><b>This boy is fine in seeing and hearing.</b>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɪʊ<span class="wrap"> </span>lʌɾɪkaː<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪eːkʰʌiː<span class="wrap"> </span>sʊnʌiː<span class="wrap"> </span>mə<span class="wrap"> </span>ʈʰiːk<span class="wrap"> </span>hʌiː</span>
</td>
<td><span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">इउ लरिका देखई सुनई म ठीक है।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td><i><b>alt.</b></i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɪ<span class="wrap"> </span>lʌɾɪkaː<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪eːkʰʌiː<span class="wrap"> </span>sʊnʌiː<span class="wrap"> </span>mə<span class="wrap"> </span>ʈʰiːk<span class="wrap"> </span>ʌhʌiː</span>
</td>
<td><i><b>alt</b></i>. <span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">इ लरिका देखई सुनई म ठीक अहै।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td rowspan="2"><b>(She) said, let (me) eat a little and give a little to this one too.</b>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">kʌɦɪn<span class="wrap"> </span>laːoː<span class="wrap"> </span>t̪ʰoːɽaː<span class="wrap"> </span>kʰaːɪ<span class="wrap"> </span>leːiː<span class="wrap"> </span>t̪ʰoːɽaː<span class="wrap"> </span>jʌhu<span class="wrap"> </span>kɘ<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪ʌɪ<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪eːiː</span>
</td>
<td><span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">कहिन, लाओ थोड़ा खाई लेई, थोड़ा यहु का दै देई।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td><i><b>alt.</b></i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">kʌɦɪn<span class="wrap"> </span>lyaːvː<span class="wrap"> </span>t̪ʰoːɽaː<span class="wrap"> </span>kʰaːɪ<span class="wrap"> </span>leːiː<span class="wrap"> </span>raːçi<span class="wrap"> </span>keː<span class="wrap"> </span>jʌnhu<span class="wrap"> </span>kɘ<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪ʌɪ<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪eːiː</span>
</td>
<td><i><b>alt</b></i>. <span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">कहिन, ल्याव थोड़ा खाई लेई, रचि के एन्हुं के दै देई।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td rowspan="2"><b>Those who go will be beaten.</b>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">d͡ʒoː<span class="wrap"> </span>d͡ʒʌɪɦʌĩ<span class="wrap"> </span>soː<span class="wrap"> </span>maːrʊ̥<span class="wrap"> </span>kʰʌɪɦʌĩ</span>
</td>
<td><span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">जो जइहैं सो मारउ खइहैं।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td><i><b>alt</b></i>. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">d͡ʒèː<span class="wrap"> </span>d͡ʒʌɪɦʌĩ<span class="wrap"> </span>soː<span class="wrap"> </span>maːr<span class="wrap"> </span>kʰʌɪɦʌĩ</span>
</td>
<td><b><i>alt</i>.</b> <span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">जे जइहैं सो मार खइहैं।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td rowspan="2"><b>Do not shoot at the birds.</b>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">cɪɾʌɪjʌn<span class="wrap"> </span>pʌɾ<span class="wrap"> </span>chʌrːaː<span class="wrap"> </span>nə<span class="wrap"> </span>cʌlaːoː</span>
</td>
<td><span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">चिरइयन पर छर्रा न चलाओ।</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#ffe;">
<td><i><b>alt</b></i>. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">cɪɾʌɪjʌn<span class="wrap"> </span>peː<span class="wrap"> </span>chʌrːaː<span class="wrap"> </span>jin<span class="wrap"> </span>cʌlaːwː</span>
</td>
<td><i><b>alt</b></i>. <span title="Awadhi-language text"><span lang="awa">चिरइयन पे छर्रा जिन चलाव।</span></span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLutgendorf1991">Lutgendorf (1991</a>:12)—Since the Ramcaritmanas is a text in the Ramayana tradition, for which the Sanskrit epic of Valmiki is the accepted archetype, it is commonly referred to simply as "the Ramayan" and many popular editions bear only this name on their spine and cover, perhaps adding above it in small print: "composed by Goswami Tulsidas".</span>

<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFRao1998" class="citation journal cs1">Rao, I. Panduranga (1998). "Review of The Beautiful Verses (Ram-Charit Manas, "Sunder-Kand" and Hanuman Chalisa of Goswami Tulsidas rendered into English verse)". <i>Indian Literature</i>. <b>41</b> (1 (183)): 240–241. <a href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/issn/0019-5804">0019-5804</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/23341337">23341337</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Indian+Literature&amp;rft.atitle=Review+of+The+Beautiful+Verses+%28Ram-Charit+Manas%2C+%22Sunder-Kand%22+and+Hanuman+Chalisa+of+Goswami+Tulsidas+rendered+into+English+verse%29&amp;rft.volume=41&amp;rft.issue=1+%28183%29&amp;rft.pages=240-241&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F23341337%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.issn=0019-5804&amp;rft.aulast=Rao&amp;rft.aufirst=I.+Panduranga&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAwadhi+language" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFOrsini2014">Orsini (2014</a>:200)—"That Brahmin <i>kathavachaks</i> were not the only tellers of the story is proved by the first Hindi vernacular adaptation of the Dasam Skandha, the Haricharit in the Chaupai Doha by Lalach Kavi, a <i>Kayastha</i> from "Hastigram" (present-day Hathgaon) near Rae Bareilly, concluded in 1530 (VS1587)."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVaudeville1990">Vaudeville (1990</a>:260)–The first editor of the <i>Kabir Granthavali</i>, S.S Das, also stresses the composite character of Kabir's language, giving examples in his introduction, of <i>vanis</i> composed in Khariboli (i.e. Standard Hindi), Rajasthani, and Panjabi, besides Awadhi.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVaudeville1990">Vaudeville (1990</a>:264)–Among the dialects or languages "melted" in the Hindavi language, the most important is Avadhi, mentioned above. The language of Kabir himself an Easterner, retains old Eastern forms, especially the old Avadhi forms.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVaudeville1990">Vaudeville (1990</a>:260)–Chaturvedi has shown that the same <i>pada</i> may be found with more characteristic Avadhi forms in the <i>Bijak</i>, with more Khari-boli in the Guru Granth and with Braj forms in the Kabir Granthavali.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVaudeville1990">Vaudeville (1990</a>:259)–According to Grierson, however, there is not a single word typical of the Bhojpuri language in the Bijak. According to him, the basic language of the Bijak is old Avadhi...</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVaudeville1990">Vaudeville (1990</a>:263)</span>
</li>
